    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2433437398" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2433437398" />
    <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea"><img alt="Amalia Gkavea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559158764p2/56789747.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2433437398?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amalia Gkavea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amalia Gkavea" href="/user/show/56789747-amalia-gkavea">Amalia Gkavea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408871751" href="/book/show/33290527-lincoln-in-the-bardo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2433437398" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10916661797687002156"><i>
  <b>‘’My mother, I said. My father. They will come shortly. To collect me’’</b>
</i><br/><br/>Death is a cruel, cynical visitor. Sometimes invited, others unexpected, many more anticipated. Death is blind to age, race, religion, kindness or evilness. He does not discriminate, he takes everyone. He is the one certain thing in the life of every living creature. An unavoidable, unquestionable snatcher. However, don’t we all desire to know what happens next? Perhaps, this is what makes us so afraid, the fear of being </span>
  <span id="freeText10916661797687002156" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>‘’My mother, I said. My father. They will come shortly. To collect me’’</b>
</i><br /><br />Death is a cruel, cynical visitor. Sometimes invited, others unexpected, many more anticipated. Death is blind to age, race, religion, kindness or evilness. He does not discriminate, he takes everyone. He is the one certain thing in the life of every living creature. An unavoidable, unquestionable snatcher. However, don’t we all desire to know what happens next? Perhaps, this is what makes us so afraid, the fear of being lost forever. Whatever it may await, I hope it will be better than the Bardo. A state where souls that haven’t been set free linger, awaiting the next spirit to join their nightmarish Chorus. The Limbo, devoid of everything. A place visited by no God, no Devil. A battlefield, a community whose agony and frustration mirrors the society of the living.<br /><br />This is where Willie, Abraham Lincoln’s son, finds himself shortly after he dies of a visceral fever. It is said that the great President would visit his child for many days, holding him and thus chaining him to a state where Willie cannot move on, keeping his soul in captivity. Saunders creates this monumental, extraordinary work around this incident and through Lincoln’s devastation, Willie’s uncertainty and confusion and the despair of all the trapped souls that become our guides in this horrifying journey, he weaves a tale of death, love and remembrance. I won’t comment on this experimental style, the theatrical format and the extraordinary ability to create a unique language for each narrator. For me, these elements aren’t important. What is important is the wealth of themes and issues that make the novel one of the most bizarre and fascinating experiences in the life of the reader.<br /><br /> <i>
  <b>‘’Many guests especially recalled the beautiful moon that shone that evening.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />If I had to choose the one thing that made this novel so powerful, it would have to be the crystal clear way in which Saunders depicts the human soul in its kindest and worst aspects. This was obvious in the various views on Abraham Lincoln expressed by the spirits and by the extracts of the press in the era of the Civil War. Demons who desired to keep people chained because they had a different skin colour regarded him as a murderer who dragged their precious sons to war. But they said nothing of the dead sons of their ‘’property’’, the raped women, the absolute loss of any trace of human dignity they inflicted on others in their bloody plantations. As we travel through the Bardo, in the chapters that describe the various stages of Willie’s wandering, we see a nation divided by conflicting aspirations and expectations. We see the souls of criminals, prostitutes, noble born people who have come to realize that Death isn’t particularly dazzled by wealth and status. Their pain, despair and struggle for acceptance echo the universe of the living. The two realms are hardly different and while the pitiful ghosts battle with themselves to retain some sort of human identity, they also battle with each other because old habits die hard, if at all. In most of the characters, the difference to their breathing counterparts is little, they hardly regret their faults, all too eager to put the blame on someone else and lure Willie into their cold company.<br /><br />Saunders writes a sublime elegy of the tremendous, frightening impact of a child’s death on the surviving family. It is a tragedy beyond words, a catastrophe that we don’t even dare to picture in our minds. The grief of good President Abraham is devastating to read, the pain of Mrs Lincoln tears through the soul. Girls that didn’t have the chance to live their love, young men who died on the battlefield, a priest who tried to fulfill his mission as best as he could, a young woman who was raped repeatedly by her master. There is so much pain and yet, the message never becomes dark or pessimistic. Even in the most brutal moments, a glimpse of hope shines through to remind us that, perhaps, if we really try to come together, to respect each other, a better world might become possible. This was Lincoln’s vision, a vision that has been viciously massacred by the majority of the presidents who have occupied the White House in recent History….Not to mention the state the world has found itself today…<br /><br />Many things have been said about <i>Lincoln In The Bardo</i>. Some may consider it verbose, pretentious, illogical. That is understandable. Not every book is for every reader. For me, this is one of the very finest moments in Literature. Not because it won the Man Booker Prize. Many winners have come and gone and I found them mediocre, forgettable. Not because of the experimental style. Many books have served this technique well, many more will do so in the future. Technicalities don’t matter. It is a masterpiece because it pays homage to the struggles and seemingly futile causes that helped made this Creation a more tolerable place to inhabit. It is a masterpiece because it is written by an author who dived deep into the human soul, found the finest and the worst in all of us and created a tale that is ferocious, sad, haunting, generous, hopeful, tender and fragile. It’s not an easy read but who wants an easy read, anyway? <br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’If I could confer with him, I know he would approve; would tell me it is right that I should go, and come back no more. He was such a noble spirit. His heart loved goodness most.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />My reviews can also be found on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://theopinionatedreaderblog.wordpress.com">https://theopinionatedreaderblog.word...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10916661797687002156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1880595851" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1880595851" />
    <a title="LeAnne: GeezerMom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7862560-leanne-geezermom"><img alt="LeAnne: GeezerMom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514579326p2/7862560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1880595851?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="LeAnne: GeezerMom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="LeAnne: GeezerMom" href="/user/show/7862560-leanne-geezermom">LeAnne: GeezerMom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7862560-leanne-geezermom?shelf=hot-mamas-book-club">hot-mamas-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7862560-leanne-geezermom?shelf=litmus-test">litmus-test</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1880595851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14724806219209154261"><b>"And if you go.. chasing rabbits,<br/>and you know you're.. going to fall<br/>Tell 'em a hookah-smoking caterpillar<br/>has given you the call."</b><br/><br/>The unusual format, like oddly punctuated and inverted lines of a play, was fine by me. It just took some adjustment, and then it was easy to read.<br/><br/>But the random, psychedelic-seeming thought trains were way too artsy for me, and the periodic sophomoric vulgarity struck me as stupid (sorry). I did not titter for an instant. There was definitely a plot to follow, but lik</span>
  <span id="freeText14724806219209154261" style="display:none"><b>"And if you go.. chasing rabbits,<br />and you know you're.. going to fall<br />Tell 'em a hookah-smoking caterpillar<br />has given you the call."</b><br /><br />The unusual format, like oddly punctuated and inverted lines of a play, was fine by me. It just took some adjustment, and then it was easy to read.<br /><br />But the random, psychedelic-seeming thought trains were way too artsy for me, and the periodic sophomoric vulgarity struck me as stupid (sorry). I did not titter for an instant. There was definitely a plot to follow, but like from the thick main trunk of a vine, there were tendrils of offshoots that obscured the main body at times. Some of these shoots were their own little stories, but some were just weedy backdrop. I'm all for setting a mood or seeing a theme evinced in different ways, although here, I just did not care about any of it.<br /><br />I did have empathy with Lincoln and his boy, but this story robbed me even of that. Others have complimented the depth of research that went into the accounts, primarily at the front end of the story, but using two books and Google can cough up many quotes and accounts, and for me (who is a boring and methodical scientist), quantity does not equal quality. As a reader, some of them were entertaining (like opposing descriptions of the moon's phase, Lincoln's appearance, etc), but a handful would have sufficed. Thankfully, these barrels full of quotations took up more space such that I did not have to spend too much reading time in the bardo.<br /><br />Oops. I've gone too far perhaps and ticked my five- and four-star friends off. But I'm not the "magical realism" type. Dull, grounded, and bound with facts, this story just isn't my thing. I do love <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/811.Yann_Martel" title="Yann Martel" rel="nofollow noopener">Yann Martel</a>'s work and that of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13450.Gabriel_Garc_a_M_rquez" title="Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">Gabriel García Márquez</a>, though. Heck, I even adore reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/117833.The_Master_and_Margarita" title="The Master and Margarita by Mikhail Bulgakov" rel="nofollow noopener">The Master and Margarita</a> every few years around Easter! Talk about the surreal! But this story went too far for me without a meaningful payoff. My 13 year old sister died pretty suddenly - within a week's time - many years ago, and maybe the deterioration of dead 11-year-old Willie speaking from the half-life added to my distaste. I felt a bit protective of and angry on behalf of President Lincoln, too. He was a real person - a heartbroken dad with a helluva stressful job. How dare we pull his memory and that of his little boy out and sandwich it with these absurd ghosts, ones with giant phalluses and multiple noses, just for our entertainment? Shall we next exhume Martin Luther King? <br /><br />The recurring matterlightblooming phenomena and Mr Bevins' ever growing number of appendages were just too ridiculous for my taste. If Edward Scissorhands or the Mad Hatter or Beetle Juice thrilled you, be my guest - you'll love this. I just do not have an active enough imagination to have taken the plunge into the bardo.<br /><br />Despite the constellations of five stars out there, this thing went dim for me at the halfway point. . I'd have to have eaten some sort of magic mushroom to have liked this...just not my taste.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14724806219209154261" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1800911301" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1800911301" />
    <a title="Diane Barnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes"><img alt="Diane Barnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329773692p2/7262060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1800911301?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Barnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Barnes" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes">Diane Barnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7262060-diane-barnes?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7262060-diane-barnes?shelf=motley-crew">motley-crew</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1800911301" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer312690104106082812">ADDITION TO REVIEW AFTER LISTENING TO AUDIO<br/><br/>This is the most unusual, incredible reading experience I have ever had. George Saunders is either a genius, or an other-worldly creature living among us and posing as an author.<br/><br/>I will leave the book description to Goodreads and the book jacket. I will only say this: if you enter this world and let yourself be carried along, you will emerge a different reader at the end. Some of you may not be able to do this, some of you may not wish to accept what is </span>
  <span id="freeText312690104106082812" style="display:none">ADDITION TO REVIEW AFTER LISTENING TO AUDIO<br /><br />This is the most unusual, incredible reading experience I have ever had. George Saunders is either a genius, or an other-worldly creature living among us and posing as an author.<br /><br />I will leave the book description to Goodreads and the book jacket. I will only say this: if you enter this world and let yourself be carried along, you will emerge a different reader at the end. Some of you may not be able to do this, some of you may not wish to accept what is presented, but those who continue will be rewarded with a better understanding of what it means to be a living, loving human being in this imperfect world.<br /><br />As I was reading I was thinking that there was no way I could follow this story on an audio book, but others reviews have convinced me that listening to this one adds another dimension to this novel. I intend to download it from my library when available, and will add to my review at that time.<br /><br />I will go out on a limb right now and predict a Pulitzer for George Saunders. And a National Book Award. Deservedly so.<br /><br />ADDITION: March 12th, 2017<br /><br />I downloaded this book on audio from my library 4 weeks after reading the print version. I am not a fan of audio books as a rule, I get impatient with the narrators and lose focus because my mind wanders as I'm listening. But after hearing all the praise for the audio, I decided to give it a chance. It proved to be just as incredible. This was more of a performance than anything else, with a different narrator for each voice, and real emotion behind the words. I used earphones and closed my eyes, and entered the graveyard again.<br />I could actually see the action, and some things I had missed the first time around were made clearer to me this time. It also helped that it is only 7 1/2 hours, so the time investment wasn't that great. Recommended in both forms, but I am glad I read the print version first.</span>
  <a data-text-id="312690104106082812" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2224593276" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2224593276" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2224593276?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WILLIAM2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WILLIAM2" href="/user/show/4684009-william2">WILLIAM2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2224593276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17090845670028204285">I avoid the G word. That’s a determination I like to put off until rereading. But this novel’s certainly masterful. I have read nothing so near perfect in some years. The narrative rides a kind of knife edge, between unbearable sorrow one moment and comic relief the next. <br/><br/>‘Almost unbearably moving’ was what <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5735.Anthony_Burgess' title='Anthony Burgess' rel='nofollow noopener'>Anthony Burgess</a> used to say about some of the better books he reviewed. I must say the same with regard to <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i>. It’s about unendurable personal loss and grief suffered by Pre</span>
  <span id="freeText17090845670028204285" style="display:none">I avoid the G word. That’s a determination I like to put off until rereading. But this novel’s certainly masterful. I have read nothing so near perfect in some years. The narrative rides a kind of knife edge, between unbearable sorrow one moment and comic relief the next. <br /><br />‘Almost unbearably moving’ was what <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5735.Anthony_Burgess" title="Anthony Burgess" rel="nofollow noopener">Anthony Burgess</a> used to say about some of the better books he reviewed. I must say the same with regard to <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i>. It’s about unendurable personal loss and grief suffered by President Abraham Lincoln when his son, Willie, died at age 11. Much of Abe’s grief is a matter of personal record, discussed in many histories and memoirs of the day. The novel is rendered in part in a cut-and-paste style reminiscent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4462369.William_S__Burroughs" title="William S. Burroughs" rel="nofollow noopener">William Burroughs</a>, though tonally it has nothing to do with his work. I do not mean to deprecate the technique, for in the pastiche sections the effect is brilliant, given that these have been assembled entirely from the works of others. <br /><br />The fictional sections, however, in the Bardo, are entirely products of the author’s imagination. The setting is Oak Hill Cemetery, in Georgetown, Washington D.C. Aside from the occasional living funeral party, it’s occupied almost entirely by the spirits of the dead, spooks: people buried there whose existences in this interstitial realm—before rebirth, according to certain schools of Tibetan Buddhism—are beset by karmic reckonings in the form of phantasmagorical torments. So there are their unendurable stories of personal loss and grief, too. In terms of emotional impact the novel’s part <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7017174.Dantes_Inferno" title="Dantes Inferno by Henrik Engel" rel="nofollow noopener">Dante’s Inferno</a> and part Juan Rulfo's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38787.Pedro_P_ramo" title="Pedro Páramo by Juan Rulfo" rel="nofollow noopener">Pedro Páramo</a>. A deeply satisfying novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17090845670028204285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2365647787" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2365647787" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2365647787?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781408871751" href="/book/show/33290527-lincoln-in-the-bardo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=2-star-reads">2-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=contemporary-lit">contemporary-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=man-booker-prize-winners">man-booker-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2365647787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9067243371104876598">It is becoming increasingly hard to create original literature these days. The fundamental problem with modern art is that everything has been done before. Writers, painters and musicians have to concoct something extraordinarily different to be new.<br/><br/>And George Saunders has done exactly that. There has never been a book quite like this before and that’s why it won the Man Booker Prize 2017. The judges for the last few years have been weighing literary originality over literary quality in my opini</span>
  <span id="freeText9067243371104876598" style="display:none">It is becoming increasingly hard to create original literature these days. The fundamental problem with modern art is that everything has been done before. Writers, painters and musicians have to concoct something extraordinarily different to be new.<br><br>And George Saunders has done exactly that. There has never been a book quite like this before and that’s why it won the Man Booker Prize 2017. The judges for the last few years have been weighing literary originality over literary quality in my opinion. <br><br>In 1917 artist Marcel Duchamp placed an upside down urinal in a gallery and labelled it art. He called it the Fountain. Such a thing was a gesture of irony and a way of arguing against the established ideas of what art can be. It’s a monumental event in art history and it also echoes what writers such as Joyce, Woolf and T.S Eliot were trying in the literary world with their experimentation. My point is: do you consider a urinal a piece of art? <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1525098898i/25458197._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Whilst <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i> is not quite a urinal, it firmly establishes that newness does not always mean greatness. <br><br>Just because something is new it does not necessarily mean it is entertaining to read or accessible for the reading public. I suppose this is the difference between high-brow and low-brow books. Critically speaking, Saunders has written something rather special here, though is it actually enjoyable? <br><br>I liked parts of it. I like the way he played with language and structure. It was very good at evoking pain, though it was simply too long. I don’t consider <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i> a bad book, though I do consider it terribly boring and repetitive. For me, I felt like I read the entire book after one hundred pages. I gained very little by continuing through to the end. No real momentum was gained and it seemed to loop in on itself with its disjointedness. I felt like I’d experienced it all very early on and the rest was just wasted words and time.<br><br>Not a book I recommend. I think it tried to hard to be different.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9067243371104876598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1879355139" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1879355139" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1879355139?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2017">read2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=location-usa-illinois">location-usa-illinois</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=around-the-usa">around-the-usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=reviewcopy">reviewcopy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=booker-winners-and-listed">booker-winners-and-listed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1879355139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2456857670606898013">Imagine the historical research approach of someone like David McCullough, and pull those details into a novel that takes place almost entirely in a graveyard, ghosts and all (picture <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2213661.The_Graveyard_Book' title='The Graveyard Book by Neil Gaiman' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Graveyard Book</a>), and you have this novel. I was lucky to receive a review copy of the audiobook from the publisher, because I think this is the preferred format for the novel. <br/><br/>Since George Saunders wrote the novel in 108 sections, with distinct voices, they decided to use 166 voices in the recording (<a href='http://time.com/4631970/george-saunders-lincoln-in-the-bardo/?iid=sr-link2' rel='nofollow noopener'>Time Magaz</a></span>
  <span id="freeText2456857670606898013" style="display:none">Imagine the historical research approach of someone like David McCullough, and pull those details into a novel that takes place almost entirely in a graveyard, ghosts and all (picture <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2213661.The_Graveyard_Book" title="The Graveyard Book by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Graveyard Book</a>), and you have this novel. I was lucky to receive a review copy of the audiobook from the publisher, because I think this is the preferred format for the novel. <br /><br />Since George Saunders wrote the novel in 108 sections, with distinct voices, they decided to use 166 voices in the recording (<a href="http://time.com/4631970/george-saunders-lincoln-in-the-bardo/?iid=sr-link2" rel="nofollow noopener">Time Magazine did a short profile of the upcoming production</a>, and you can <a href="http://www.penguinrandomhouseaudio.com/book/231506/lincoln-in-the-bardo/?ref=PRH9D348A9BAA&amp;hootPostID=485b359347de32af2c18e6ccc85686c2" rel="nofollow noopener">listen to an excerpt on the publisher site</a>.)<br /><br />Nick Offerman and David Sedaris, along with George Saunders, are three primary voices (although I thought Sedaris was Holly Hunter until after I'd finished, despite having heard him narrate his own books) and a cast of friends, stars, and family fill out the rest. Some voices are heard only one time, reading a letter or fact from what sounds like real sources, and I imagine some are, some aren't. That is a bit confusing in the beginning, until you get into the rhythm of the novel. It's enough to know that you don't need to remember the voices in conjunction with their names, so they can pass through your mind. <br /><br />Some of the time the multiple voices seem to just be providing context, but often they are playing with the narrative of context, some remembering a full moon, others remembering a cloudy night, others remembering a crescent, etc. These tiny excerpts are often followed by the narrator with an abbreviation I had not heard, so here's a hint: "Op. cit." refers to a longer bibliography or a previously mentioned citation. I wish they had left that out of the audio because I had no idea what it was for most of my listening experience. I had looked up opsit, opsid, oppsid, upsid, and every other combination until I found it. It's a minor thing but gets used so frequently with all the tiny bits, that it drove me to distraction!<br /><br />Overall the novel is pretty fragmented, and I found I did better in comprehending it when listening for long spans of time, like the five hours I spent in the car yesterday. I am not sure what kind of novel I expected Saunders to write for his first published longer work, but I did not expect something quite so simultaneously historical and experimental!<br /><br />The highlight for me has to be Nick Offerman though. He is an excellent narrator and now I want to go back and listen to more of his productions. More, please!<br /><br />ETA: Changed my 4 stars to 5 the more I thought about it.<br /><br />One more ETA: love or hate it, this sucker just won the Man Booker Prize!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2456857670606898013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1874986939" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1874986939" />
    <a title="Darwin8u" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u"><img alt="Darwin8u" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547440402p2/2294090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1874986939?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=aere-perennius">aere-perennius</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1874986939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10321634806649822333"><i>"He came out of nothingness, took form, was loved, was always bound to return to nothingness."</i><br/>- George Saunders, Lincoln in the Bardo<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487397066i/22008393._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='314' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Again, I find myself wandering at night alone, reading grief literature. I'm not sure if I have just accidentally stumbled on my own special vein of grief literature or if this dark path has suddenly become more popular (<i>"to hell with erotic fiction, let us read tales of the sad survivors"</i>). But, here I am, writing another transuding review of another sad book. No.</span>
  <span id="freeText10321634806649822333" style="display:none"><i>"He came out of nothingness, took form, was loved, was always bound to return to nothingness."</i><br>- George Saunders, Lincoln in the Bardo<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487397066i/22008393._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="314" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Again, I find myself wandering at night alone, reading grief literature. I'm not sure if I have just accidentally stumbled on my own special vein of grief literature or if this dark path has suddenly become more popular (<i>"to hell with erotic fiction, let us read tales of the sad survivors"</i>). But, here I am, writing another transuding review of another sad book. No. Not sad. Certainly, it deals with sadness. Death and sadness. Certainly. But it is hard to contain Saunders. His stories were never easily boxed, labeled, or restrained. They seemed to crawl under doors, slip through walls, escape the clutches of easy definition. This novel, his first, is also hard to easily categorize. It is historical fiction in that its primary characters (Abraham Lincoln and his dead son William) are historical. But the rest of the characters (and there is an army of characters) are mostly fictional or fictionalized. The book is also historical in the sense that Saunders uses, in an unusual way, historical writings about Lincoln as almost a Greek chorus. There are chapters where a repetition of historical voices, like a series of paintings by Giorgio Morandi (where he paints the same few household objects in slightly different constellations) creates one image out of many eyes. All of these studies, given at different times seem to not confuse the subject but illuminate the subject. So when, in this book, Lincoln's eyes are described by different historical voices as:<br><br><i>"His eyes dark grey, clear, very expressive, and varying with every mood"</i><br>- Arnold, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1199094.The_Life_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="The Life of Abraham Lincoln by Isaac Newton Arnold" rel="nofollow noopener">The Life of Abraham Lincoln</a><br><br><i>"His eyes were bright, keen, and a luminous grey color."</i><br>- Ostendorf, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6357324.Lincoln_s_Photographs___A_Complete_Album" title="Lincoln's Photographs - A Complete Album by Lloyd Ostendorf" rel="nofollow noopener">Lincoln's Photographs - A Complete Album</a><br><br><i>"Grey-brown eyes sunken under thick eyebrows, and as though encircled by deep and dark wrinkles."</i><br>- Marquis de Chambrun, "Personal Recollections of Mr. Lincoln"<br><br><i>"His eyes were a bluish-brown."</i><br>- Wilson &amp; Davis, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/113583.Herndon_s_Informants_Letters__Interviews__and_Statements_about_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Herndon's Informants Letters, Interviews, and Statements about Abraham Lincoln by Douglas L. Wilson" rel="nofollow noopener">Herndon's Informants: Letters, Interviews, and Statements about Abraham Lincoln</a><br><br><i>"The saddest eyes of any human being that I have ever seen."</i><br>Shenk, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106591.Lincoln_s_Melancholy_How_Depression_Challenged_a_President_and_Fueled_His_Greatness" title="Lincoln's Melancholy How Depression Challenged a President and Fueled His Greatness by Joshua Wolf Shenk" rel="nofollow noopener">Lincoln's Melancholy: How Depression Challenged a President and Fueled His Greatness</a><br><br>we get a moving picture. Many pictures, flickering, on-after-another form a living, moving portrait of the MAN.<br><br>This technique of borrowing the same description of Lincoln or of an event of Lincoln's life and telling it again and again becomes almost a mantra that pushes the reader back into reality. Shows the reader how things exist in a multitude of ways. How reality might be ALL the experiences of a person; past, present and future and seen by everyone. It also seduces the reader and lulls the reader into a laconic, dream-like state as the strangeness of the novels swirls around. These chapters become an incantation, a rite, a ritual, a dirge.<br><br>One of the small gems I loved from this novel is one that won't mean much to anyone else but me. One of the dead slaves, one of the "Black Beasts", the "Damnable savages" also stuck in purgatory shares my name, or my last name at least. I'm tribal enough that I enjoy that. I like the serendipity of discovering one of the best characters, with one of the best "voices", "the sweetest f____er, but talks so G____ complicated" has my last name. Perfect. That is the best part of this book, the gist really. That it is IN others and THROUGH others and HELPING others that we discover our own worth. We are saved IN and BY our empathy. As we see the world through slave and soldier's eyes. As we grapple with the suffering of all, the pain of all, we redeem and save each other. Lovely.<br><br>The danger with grief literature is it can so, so easily slip into sentimentality. It can quickly slide into sap and pretentiousness. Grief is hard. It is real. It also something, like sex, that is fraught and dangerous to write about. Saunders nails it. He is able to walk THROUGH the valley and WITH the shadows and fear no evil, because tonight, for me, Saunders is the baddest son-of-a-bitch in the bardo</span>
  <a data-text-id="10321634806649822333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1985494049" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1985494049" />
    <a title="Jaline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline"><img alt="Jaline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530498058p2/7234171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1985494049?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jaline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jaline" href="/user/show/7234171-jaline">Jaline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7234171-jaline?shelf=xx2017-completed">xx2017-completed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1985494049" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7187512708357695057">This is an intriguing book; one that is very inventive and yet its basic premise is based on strong possibilities, if not probabilities. There are brief historical excerpts throughout from various sources that are amazing in that they outline stronger than ever that “eye witness” testimony is pretty much wasted without a camera to back it up. For example, on a historically memorable night 5 or 10 people can look at the same night sky and see no moon at all, or a moon – but in about 5 or 6 differ</span>
  <span id="freeText7187512708357695057" style="display:none">This is an intriguing book; one that is very inventive and yet its basic premise is based on strong possibilities, if not probabilities. There are brief historical excerpts throughout from various sources that are amazing in that they outline stronger than ever that “eye witness” testimony is pretty much wasted without a camera to back it up. For example, on a historically memorable night 5 or 10 people can look at the same night sky and see no moon at all, or a moon – but in about 5 or 6 different colors and shapes. They can also look at a man’s eyes and see different colors and different expressions. The subjectivity and deception of our senses are brought to life more in this book than any other I have read.<br /><br />Abraham Lincoln’s young son, Willie, becomes ill and dies. The Lincoln family are devastated by the loss of their 11 year old boy. Unknowingly, President Lincoln’s deep pain of mourning and promises made at his son’s interment site are anchoring young Willie and preventing him from leaving the state of Bardo and moving on.<br /><br />In Tibetan Buddhism, Bardo is comprised of several different levels of consciousness that a soul after death ideally moves through to reach the afterlife or next life entry level. There are ways to prepare oneself for these stages while still living, but most souls will have to find their way through the various stages on their own. These stages are recognizable in the various “grounded” souls that Willie comes into contact with. The dialogues here are funny, poignant, and sad – separately and all at once. These souls tell their stories throughout the book – some clearer than others. Sometimes when they get ‘stuck’ in their story they receive prompting from others who have heard their stories literally thousands of times before and have them memorized.<br /><br />The storyline clips back and forth between President Lincoln, the small excerpts from his admirers and detractors, and the various souls in the graveyard, all of them stuck in some level of Bardo that is still attached to earthly concerns.<br /><br />There are so many aspects of this book that impressed me, and while I couldn’t help but feel the intense sadness of President Lincoln and his family, I was also entertained by the stories and the wide variety of states of consciousness of the souls in Bardo. I actually feel guilty saying that – but some of the exchanges were just plain funny.<br /><br />This is an impressive book for so many reasons and in the end, I was left with the kind of feeling you get when walking out of a theatre after watching an especially good movie. The bemused smile on the face, the eyes half in this world and half still seeing parts of the movie, the mind at rest yet still processing what it just witnessed. I highly recommend this book to anyone who loves that “after-movie” feeling with none of the accompanying partial deafness.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7187512708357695057" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1978156779" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1978156779" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/236411-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291695844p2/236411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1978156779?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/236411-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1978156779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15875034827445415862">You know those half-awake, half-asleep dreams where you’re working through your problems of the day? The first wakeful moments in the shower usually let you know that any solutions you thought might apply were pure nonsense. Even more often you realize the things you were thinking about weren’t really problems anyway – it was all just anxiety for the hell of it. Anyway, last night I went to bed thinking about what I might say about this celebrated new Saunders book I just read. Even as I was fal</span>
  <span id="freeText15875034827445415862" style="display:none">You know those half-awake, half-asleep dreams where you’re working through your problems of the day? The first wakeful moments in the shower usually let you know that any solutions you thought might apply were pure nonsense. Even more often you realize the things you were thinking about weren’t really problems anyway – it was all just anxiety for the hell of it. Anyway, last night I went to bed thinking about what I might say about this celebrated new Saunders book I just read. Even as I was falling asleep, I knew in the light of day this would end up being one of those non-problems. But then I started thinking about a request I got at work to give a presentation about statistical modeling to the 50 or so technologists in our firm. These are clever people – the types who could write episodes of <i>Silicon Valley</i> - so, warily, I began preparing slides in my head that might interest them. As soon as the REM sleep kicked in, I guess I conflated my two goals. I came up with mathematical breakdowns of <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i> instead. Of course, the first drops of water from the shower should have told me to abandon such thoughts, but I was hard up for an angle as it was, which is a long introduction for what you’re about to see. [<i>Note to self: Don’t mention Goodreads reviews in stats preso.</i>]<br /><br />I might as well start with the star rating. The input variables in this case, all ranging from 0 (lowest) to 1 (highest) were:<br /><blockquote>Quality of writing = 0.88<br />Creativity in structuring = 0.93<br />Depth of probes into character = 0.48<br />Exploration of themes = 0.85<br />Achievement versus expectations = 0.27</blockquote><br />Applying equal additive weights to each variable we get:<br /><blockquote>Rating = 0.88 + 0.93 + 0.48 + 0.85 + 0.27 = 3.41</blockquote><br />I’d read several rave reviews which enticed me. And the premise (Lincoln’s visits to the cemetery after his dear son Willie died at which time and place the transitioning souls there in the bardo encountered him) sounded interesting, too, even if a tad gimmicky. The multiplicity of stories and situations were a chance to place all of humankind on a platter for Lincoln (and us) to ingest. The bardo is a Buddhist concept pertaining to the state of being between death and rebirth – presumably a time of reflection and atonement. As Saunders envisioned it, the cross-section of inhabitants was a wide and noisy one. Still, a more enlightened understanding of one another’s ways was possible. Empathy and acceptance were the apparent goals. I give Saunders plenty of credit for highlighting these themes. (I give him even more credit for this <a>commencement speech</a> he gave a few years ago, making a related case for kindness.) My only disappointment was that the individual stories seemed too diffuse and too thin to really permeate. <br /><br />The book touched briefly on what life must have been like for Lincoln at that point. For someone who was said to be unusually kindhearted and sympathetic to begin with, the grief of Willie’s death along with the weight of the war were almost more than he could bear. Even so, as his country’s leader he knew he couldn’t wallow. Instead, he had to remind himself of the moral math (though he might not have thought of it in quite those terms) of suffering. His time in the cemetery reminded him that everyone has hardships of some kind at some time, some far worse than others. His job was to make decisions to minimize the cumulative sum of it. So, which is the smaller amount?<br /><blockquote>Cumulative suffering given war = Σ Misery[i, t | war] (summed across all individuals i, and future episodes t)</blockquote><br />or<br /><blockquote>Cumulative suffering given no war = Σ Misery[i, t | no war] (summed across all individuals i, and future episodes t)</blockquote><br />The misery of slavery was given a suitably high weight, of course. In fact, Lincoln’s empathy for all was given a representational boost when certain of the souls discovered they could inhabit him and cross-absorb experiences – literally (for purposes of the story) walking a mile in his shoes. A former slave was notably included.<br /><br />Ancillary mathematical discussion: Certain traits seem to combine additively to produce a given effect. Others are multiplicative. In the example that comes to mind regarding Lincoln (hagiography alert), I’m positing an exponential relationship.<br /><blockquote>Power ^ Empathy = Greatness</blockquote><br />One of the thornier issues we face as we endeavor to empathize is the amount of dispensation to assign. It’s easy to say that some higher moral authority has that job, but I still think the old “free will vs. determinism” debate is a good one. Several in the bardo argued that they may have done bad things, but were compelled by their natures and circumstances to do so. Expressing it in an equation, we might get something like this:<br /><blockquote>Actions and Attitudes = function(Genetics, Brain chemistry, Upbringing, Outside influences like friends or books, Physical needs) + Residual</blockquote><br />The residual in this model is the part of our actions and attitudes that cannot be explained by the drivers. It’s what I’m imagining free will to be. So the question is, what portion, if any, is to be labeled a choice, superseding what the assigned factors would otherwise dictate? Is that what we’re to be judged by?<br /><br />I was coached once, when putting together a presentation, to go easy on the equations. Eyes for another half your audience will glaze over for each additional one you include. I guess he was saying:<br /><blockquote>Remaining interest = Original interest * (0.5 ^ # of equations)</blockquote><br />How many of you does that leave? I’m not even sure I can include myself, having mentally checked out while summing the miseries of those potentially reading this review of a sort.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15875034827445415862" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1758207397" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1758207397" />
    <a title="Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6354112-perry"><img alt="Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588528919p2/6354112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1758207397?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry" href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6354112-perry?shelf=books-most-loved">books-most-loved</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6354112-perry?shelf=mina-favoritb%C3%B6cker">mina-favoritböcker</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6354112-perry?shelf=credshelf">credshelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1758207397" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14216418847086397893"><b>Like a cosmic chorus in 'cordial unison' under brocades of blue and a rain of red glare</b><br/><br/>George Saunders' brilliant literary achievement is the ideal book for the Fourth of July in profoundly reminding us of our union as citizens of the United States of America, this great nation created by our forefathers' Declaration of Independence from the "<i>absolute Despotism</i>," "<i>long train of abuses and usurpations</i>" and "<i>invasions on the rights of the people</i>" by the then "King of Great Britain," our democracy </span>
  <span id="freeText14216418847086397893" style="display:none"><b>Like a cosmic chorus in 'cordial unison' under brocades of blue and a rain of red glare</b><br /><br />George Saunders' brilliant literary achievement is the ideal book for the Fourth of July in profoundly reminding us of our union as citizens of the United States of America, this great nation created by our forefathers' Declaration of Independence from the "<i>absolute Despotism</i>," "<i>long train of abuses and usurpations</i>" and "<i>invasions on the rights of the people</i>" by the then "King of Great Britain," our democracy founded upon principles of equality and each individual's endowment "<i>by [our] Creator with certain unalienable rights, [such as] Life, Liberty and the pursuit of Happiness</i>." And, it provides just as important a remembrance of how our country was nearly crushed, but ultimately was forged, by the crucible of a civil war that cost the lives of over 620,000 young men (in perspective, a number that today would proportionally be roughly six and a half million). <br /><br />More significant than these, through a fictional rendering of our President Abraham Lincoln's loss of his 11-year-old son Willie, the novel elegantly recalls our connectedness, despite our many differences, through our shared human experience of love, family and the anguished grief we each suffer in losing those we love.<br /><br />In February 1862, close to a year after President Lincoln's inauguration and ten months after the start of the Civil War, he lost his son Willie to typhoid fever. Author George Saunders, primarily known for his satirical short stories, speculates that President Lincoln's revival in the face of a human being's greatest loss--of his child--prepared him especially to pull the nation through the jaws of darkness and the legion losses suffered by families on both sides of the Civil War. <br /><br />Saunders crafts three vibrant, compelling characters from differing backgrounds to drive the novel's narrative in Georgetown's Oak Hill cemetery, in the "bardo" (Tibetan for limbo between the world and the eternal afterlife). Hans Vollman is a charmingly gruff printer who carries about in a tumescent state [existing as he died, just prior to consummating his marriage to a wife twenty years his junior]; Roger Bevins, an Orphean suicide with a bounty of extra eyes, hands, ears, noses that pop out when he becomes agitated or excited; and, Reverend Everly Thomas, a sage man of the cloth whose mouth is cast permanently in an "O" of fright.<br /><br />This trio is joined by a host of other colorful characters as they chaperone young Willie, left behind in the bardo instead of ascending because he wanted to see his dad once more.<br /><br />Saunders seamlessly intersperses the novel's rich and intellectually stimulating dialogue with brief chapters containing excerpts from news accounts, memoirs, diaries and biographies of the time, which display the harsh tenor of the public outcry in the months after Lincoln assumed the presidency, when he had no clear plan for resolving the nascent war or the conflicts leading to the southern states' secessions, including: "<i>The Presdt is an idiot</i>," George B. McClellan; "<i>Vain, weak, puerile, hypocritical... By all odds, the weakest man who has ever been elected</i>," Sherrard Clemens; and, "<i>Will go down to posterity as the man who could not ... understand the circumstances and interests of his country... who plunged his country into a great war without a plan; who failed without excuse, and fell without a friend</i>," London Morning Post.<br /><br />The novel largely enamors with its punchy tone containing humor to prompt a few laughs loud enough to wake the family. The narrative tightens for a short stretch when departed slaves from outside the segregated cemetery travel to Oak Hill to see the President, only to be met by resistance from a violent, racist faction led by a bombastic Confederate officer. <br /><br />I cannot recall a novel so manifesting that which unites us as a country and as human beings: "<i>At the core of each lay suffering; our eventual end, the many losses we must experience on the way to that end.</i>" At the same time, the novel inspires in its portrayal of the human spirit's resilience and perseverance. Abraham Lincoln, generally acknowledged as the country's greatest president, who had been "<i>broken, awed, humbled, diminished</i>" by his son's death and roundly ridiculed as "<i>a person of very inferior cast of character, wholly unequal to the crisis</i>," rises from the ruins to remake himself and hold together a nation seemingly torn asunder. <br /><br />The bardo's voices range widely from farmers and soldiers to priests and suicides, as emblematic of the diversity of opinions and backgrounds of our country's departed in 1861. Saunders writes each tone as valued and like he's conducting a symphony, with the result being a sort of cosmic chorus in "cordial unison"* under brocades of blue, twinkling white willows and a rain of red glare, celebrating the human spirit and similarities that can unite our nation. <br /><br />By novel's end, the President's final goodbye to the body of his son can leave one undone; for others, the trio's farewell aria might do just as well, by lacing the reader with the unalloyed beauty of simple things in life that we take for granted as if our days here are limitless. Bevins, the young erudite poet, captures it best as he contemplates leaving the world behind: "<i>the things of the world were strong with me still...such as... cold water from a tin jug; toweling off one's clinging shirt post-June rain... Pearls, rags, buttons... The way a moistness in the eye will blur a field of stars... writing one's beloved's name upon a frosted window with a gloved finger...</i>"<br /><br />It is no stretch to predict that time will bestow this novel with the status of a "modern classic," one that is studied in literature classes when your children's children attend grade school. "Lincoln in the Bardo" represents a memorial to those who have gone before us, a tribute to President Abraham Lincoln, and a celebration of Americans' spirit to rise from defeats and our resolve to endure and succeed.<br /><br /><br />*Thomas Paine used the phrase "cordial unison" in his 1791 "Rights of Man" to describe the United States' form of government as putting a premium on equality and the unalienable rights of all. [Unfortunately, it wasn't until over a century later that anyone but white men were provided any (or most) of these rights].</span>
  <a data-text-id="14216418847086397893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1636217822" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1636217822" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1826682-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252295186p2/1826682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1636217822?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/1826682-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=allegory">allegory</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=america">america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=author-i-d-most-like-to-meet">author-i-d-most-like-to-meet</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=funny">funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1826682-trish?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1636217822" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5632480928256703041">The form of this novel is what readers will notice first. It begins as a series of quotes from reporters’ notebooks, eyewitness accounts, historians using original sources, and we must assume, Civil War-era gossip rags, describing an 1862 White House party which a thousand or more people attended. To say the affair was elaborate understates the case. Apparently when a thousand hungry guests descended on the tables of food, the quantity was such that it looked untouched after the assault. <br/><br/>Some of</span>
  <span id="freeText5632480928256703041" style="display:none">The form of this novel is what readers will notice first. It begins as a series of quotes from reporters’ notebooks, eyewitness accounts, historians using original sources, and we must assume, Civil War-era gossip rags, describing an 1862 White House party which a thousand or more people attended. To say the affair was elaborate understates the case. Apparently when a thousand hungry guests descended on the tables of food, the quantity was such that it looked untouched after the assault. <br /><br />Some of the reports mention that this lavish dinner party was going on during the war between the states (1862), and while Lincoln’s favorite son, Willie, lay dying upstairs, probably of typhoid. Some accounts criticize rather than report. Some are clearly inaccurate: “There was a large moon”; or “there was no moon.” Surely there can be no argument about these truths; one of the accounts must be untrue.<br /><br />As the novel progresses, it changes form. The reportage becomes a chorus, as voices of the bardo—that state of existence between death and rebirth—declaim and consider the suffering of Lincoln as he contemplates his son’s death. Father and son (who’d been but a child!) had been intimates, together at every opportunity, heads often canted towards one another in deep conversation. The voices of the bardo are bawdy, rowdy, yet weirdly profound in their discussion of how fleeting life and how final death and what we learn in the course of a life and what we learn only when we’ve lost it all. <br /><br />A bardo implies rebirth, but these characters appear to be looking only to escape everlasting nothingness, and enjoy discussing and dissecting the lives of others. Occasionally one of the dead will enjoy a peek at their future (best) selves, which they hadn’t the time or the opportunity to attain. It can be quite moving as each considers his or her life. And here, amidst the humor and tragedy and regret and outright joy—the <i>stuff</i> of life—resides the talent of George Saunders, as he tries to reach his best self, whether in love, work, or understanding.<br /><br />It’s difficult to believe this is Saunders’ first published novel, and yet that is its designation. It doesn’t even seem like a novel, but immediately brings to mind a radio show, something meant to be spoken aloud, in its many and varied voices. The thread of the novel is not difficult to follow like some avant-garde works, though one may wonder if Lincoln’s sorrow at the death of Willie is all Saunders meant to convey. I think not.<br /><br />I think there is another step that Saunders wants us to take: that the spirits of the bardo (how it begins to sound like bordello, the more we know of it!) influenced Lincoln when his son died, giving him insight, empathy, and the strength to carry on with his responsibilities, and to bear his personal sorrow, but also the sorrows of a nation at war. We have yet to meet the man who could have stood it alone. <blockquote>"His mind was freshly inclined toward <i>sorrow</i>; toward the fact that the world was full of sorrow; that everyone labored under some burden of sorrow; that all were suffering; that whatever way one took in this world, one must try to remember that all were suffering (none content; all wronged, neglected, overlooked, misunderstood), and therefore one must do what one could to lighten the load of those with whom one came into contact…We must try to see one another in this way…As suffering, limited beings…Perennially outmatched by circumstance, inadequately endowed with compensatory graces…And yet…Our grief must be defeated; it must not become our master, and make us ineffective…We must, to do the maximum good, bring the thing to its swiftest halt and…Kill more efficiently…Must end suffering by causing more suffering…His heart dropped at the thought of the killing…"</blockquote>So, we <i>must</i> fight, if fighting is required, to defeat wherever oppression exists. We must work together, and we’ll need all the help we can get from those who have glimpsed truth, and the value of kindness. <br /><br />In a <a href="http://www.wnyc.org/story/george-saunderss-lincoln/" rel="nofollow noopener">radio podcast with David Remnick</a>, editor of <i>The New Yorker</i>, Saunders tells us that in his research he discovers that Lincoln could have negotiated an end to the war in 1862 when the casualty levels were terrifically high. He must have wanted to end the slaughter so desperately, but one requirement of the agreement would have been to return the slaves to the South, and Lincoln simply refused. The black people who make an appearance in this novel live cruelly unfair and insecure lives.<br /><br />One could make the case that a novel of this kind is not unprecedented. Think of the ancient Greeks with their choruses of wise and not-so-wise men; Italy’s Dante with his examination of the good or bad we do in life affecting our placement in the afterlife; England’s Shakespeare with his oft-found articulate spirits remarking on the action; Ireland’s Beckett (and his influence Joyce) for language and the insight wrapped in foolishness; America’s Barth and Mamet for exactitude and a deep, abiding humor when rationality might suggest despair.<br /><br />The rich variety of voices in this novel are captured in the audio production of this book. In <a href="http://time.com/4631970/george-saunders-lincoln-in-the-bardo/" rel="nofollow noopener">an interview published in time.com</a>, Saunders explains how the Penguin Random House team worked with him (kudos, everyone) to get the requisite 166 voices, including famous stage and screen actors like David Sedaris, Ben Stiller, Lena Dunham, among others, to speak the parts so that it sounds like the “American chorale” Saunders was trying to convey.<br /><br />At the same time, I found it helpful to have a written text to clarify Saunders’ experimental form which uses footnotes interspersed with conversation among ghosts. I adored what Saunders was able to tell us from his advanced age of 58 years—the stuff about not doing anything you can’t adequately explain to heaven’s gatekeepers, and how “it wasn’t my fault” actually isn’t much of a defense when one has been lingering in the afterworld for more than fifty years, unable to convince even a bleeding-heart saint that one wasn’t a douche that time.<br /><br /><a href="http://bit.ly/2k5sesu" rel="nofollow noopener">Links to an audio clip of this production</a> are posted on my blog, along with a short, three-minute <i>New Yorker Video</i> about Saunders and his writing life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5632480928256703041" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1952942908?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>
