        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1952942908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17226050311157365343">A reader of George Saunders’ 2017 novel Lincoln in the Bardo will make immediate comparisons to Thornton Wilder’s 1938 drama <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/205476.Our_Town' title='Our Town by Thornton Wilder' rel='nofollow noopener'>Our Town</a> and Edgar Lee Master’s 1915 book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/292668.Spoon_River_Anthology' title='Spoon River Anthology by Edgar Lee Masters' rel='nofollow noopener'>Spoon River Anthology</a> as well as observing references to Dante’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15645.Inferno__The_Divine_Comedy__1_' title='Inferno (The Divine Comedy #1) by Dante Alighieri' rel='nofollow noopener'>Inferno</a> and the Bible.<br/><br/>Saunders, known for such unique and original works as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13641208.Tenth_of_December' title='Tenth of December by George Saunders' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tenth of December</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28747.CivilWarLand_in_Bad_Decline' title='CivilWarLand in Bad Decline by George Saunders' rel='nofollow noopener'>CivilWarLand in Bad Decline</a>, has again stretched the limits of creative writing and has here produced a work of fiction unlike any other. Extensively researched (though I</span>
  <span id="freeText17226050311157365343" style="display:none">A reader of George Saunders’ 2017 novel Lincoln in the Bardo will make immediate comparisons to Thornton Wilder’s 1938 drama <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/205476.Our_Town" title="Our Town by Thornton Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">Our Town</a> and Edgar Lee Master’s 1915 book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/292668.Spoon_River_Anthology" title="Spoon River Anthology by Edgar Lee Masters" rel="nofollow noopener">Spoon River Anthology</a> as well as observing references to Dante’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15645.Inferno__The_Divine_Comedy__1_" title="Inferno (The Divine Comedy #1) by Dante Alighieri" rel="nofollow noopener">Inferno</a> and the Bible.<br><br>Saunders, known for such unique and original works as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13641208.Tenth_of_December" title="Tenth of December by George Saunders" rel="nofollow noopener">Tenth of December</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28747.CivilWarLand_in_Bad_Decline" title="CivilWarLand in Bad Decline by George Saunders" rel="nofollow noopener">CivilWarLand in Bad Decline</a>, has again stretched the limits of creative writing and has here produced a work of fiction unlike any other. Extensively researched (though I suspect some of the references cited were also fictitious) about President Lincoln and the death of the Lincolns’ young son Willie during the early stages of the Civil War, Saunders explores themes of the afterlife, family, loyalty, and community. <br><br>Some readers may also see similarities with Neil Gaiman’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2213661.The_Graveyard_Book" title="The Graveyard Book by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Graveyard Book</a>, and the setting is comparable, but whereas Gaiman crafted a paranormal retelling of Kipling’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77270.The_Jungle_Book" title="The Jungle Book by Rudyard Kipling" rel="nofollow noopener">The Jungle Book</a>, Saunders has created a metaphysical examination of the supernatural and the spiritual in an absurdist context.<br><br>Original and entertaining it can be difficult to follow in places but for fans of experimental writing, this is a special work. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1550077315i/27058053.jpg" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1656331152" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1656331152" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2228181-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507660688p2/2228181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1656331152?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/2228181-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2228181-jill?shelf=best-of-2016">best-of-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1656331152" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15213866608759365872">One of my great passions in life is reading – and reviewing – books. But how to review this book? It renders me speechless and. I almost feel compelled to reduce my review to two words: “Read it.”<br/><br/>Years ago, I learned, while visiting the Lincoln Museum in Springfield, Illinois, that Abraham Lincoln was so prostrated by grief after the death of his favorite son Willie that he visited the crypt for months afterwards, opening the coffin and stroking the face and hair of his deceased son. It’s a maca</span>
  <span id="freeText15213866608759365872" style="display:none">One of my great passions in life is reading – and reviewing – books. But how to review this book? It renders me speechless and. I almost feel compelled to reduce my review to two words: “Read it.”<br /><br />Years ago, I learned, while visiting the Lincoln Museum in Springfield, Illinois, that Abraham Lincoln was so prostrated by grief after the death of his favorite son Willie that he visited the crypt for months afterwards, opening the coffin and stroking the face and hair of his deceased son. It’s a macabre image but one that is sears at the very heart of anyone who has lost a loved one.<br /><br />This image is the seed from which Lincoln in the Bardo sprouts. The Bardo, in Tibetian Buddhism, is a sort of limbo state between death and rebirth into the next life. Willie lingers in that state, unable to move forward, while his father languishes in his own bardo: needing to take the helm and steer the country forward while feeling near paralyzed by his despondency.<br /><br />The book is peopled by ghosts except for three of the living: the president, the night watchman, and a woman in a home across from the graveyard. Using a Greek chorus approach, many characters speak in turn – Willie and many of his fellow deceased who cannot move on and two of the three living characters. We never hear Abraham Lincoln’s own voice.<br /><br />Even more remarkably, the book not only intertwines and living and the dead, it also intertweaves history. There are direct quotes from the time (all annotated) as well as fictional quotes from the time (also “annotated”) and then there are the voices. Only the most precise historian would be able to decipher what is real and what is invented.<br /><br />Yet it almost doesn’t matter because the book is not just – or even primarily -- about Abraham and Willie. It is about Every Man. And here is where it becomes remarkable. As the chorus reminds us: “Though on the surface it seemed every person was different, this was not true. At the core of each lay suffering, our eventual end, the many losses we must experience on the way to that end. We must try to see each other in this way. As suffering, limited beings—Perennially outmatched by circumstance, inadequately endowed with compensatory graces.”<br /><br />George Saunders, in soaring and lyrical prose that sometimes approaches poetry, advances the theme that whatever happens to us after we die, it won’t be entirely unconnected to what we are while living. By the end of the novel, I was convinced that this was not a death story. It’s a love story – a love story for humanity and all that interconnects us.<br /><br />One of the characters says, “One must be constantly looking for opportunities to tell one’s story.” To understand our own story and to have the understanding and grace to tell it is truly what matters. This may be one of my favorite contemporary books ever.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15213866608759365872" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1656331152">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1877216279" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1877216279" />
    <a title="Matthew Quann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann"><img alt="Matthew Quann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528393487p2/22699228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1877216279?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Quann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Quann" href="/user/show/22699228-matthew-quann">Matthew Quann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">avant-garde, black humour fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=booker-prize">booker-prize</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=man-booker-shortlist-2017">man-booker-shortlist-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22699228-matthew-quann?shelf=golden-booker">golden-booker</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1877216279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6629060411944032297"><b>Update: Booker Prize Winner 2017, a well deserved win for this excellent novel!</b><br/> <br/>I came upon <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i> as someone comes upon a house on fire—tentatively. Placing a hand to the embossed dust jacket and turning deckle-edged pages at random filled me with the sphincter-tightening dread, which I have previously equated to looking down at the earth from a significant height. It seemed as if the book were more screenplay than proper novel, and I had no interest in dawdling amidst incomprehen</span>
  <span id="freeText6629060411944032297" style="display:none"><b>Update: Booker Prize Winner 2017, a well deserved win for this excellent novel!</b><br /> <br />I came upon <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i> as someone comes upon a house on fire—tentatively. Placing a hand to the embossed dust jacket and turning deckle-edged pages at random filled me with the sphincter-tightening dread, which I have previously equated to looking down at the earth from a significant height. It seemed as if the book were more screenplay than proper novel, and I had no interest in dawdling amidst incomprehensible and unintelligible style. Yet, I decided to persevere in order to expand my horizons and develop—<br /><i>matthew </i><br /><br />Awww, cut the shit, will ya? We all know ya did nothin’ but listen to the audiobook because you, how did he put it?<br /><i>mr.mcdowell</i><br /><br />“I am unable to bear the difficulties of reading a postmodern work during my studies.”<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />Yeah, what’s Mr. Quann said. <br /><i>mr.mcdowell</i><br /><br />Gentlemen, that is quite enough. Especially from two who so enjoyed the listening experience!<br /><i>matthew </i><br /><br />We got no problem with the book, we just got issue with you trying to play it off like you read the goddamned thing.<br /><i>mr.mcdowell</i><br /><br />I agree that misrepresentation of your consumption of the novel would lead others astray. Though there has been some data to suggest that reading and listening to an audiobook provide the same experience and value.<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />While I might concede that the quotidian audiobook adaptation might provide the same benefits as that of the paper copy, <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i> is no such commonplace aural production.<br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />Indeed, Matthew brings up a salient point. This is not what you would expect from most audiobooks.<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />Enough with the goddamned ten-dollar bullshit words. The people don’t want to read no review of a book that makes them pick up another goddamned book to figure out what’s being said. Let me tell it to you like it is.<br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />By all means.<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />And so it begins again.<br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />Ya see, there’s a lot of goddamned people in this book. All ghosts, or mostly, it’s pretty goddamned confusing from the get-go and I suggested getting the hell outta the way of something so shitting confusing, grabbing a couple beers and sitting back to some goddamned television. But no, no, these chumps wanted to keep on listening having no idea what’s what for the first hour. This isn’t what it’s like with goddamned television. Nothing like the television to get you one man kicking another man, or punching another in the teeth, occasionally a bit of nudity—<br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />To the point, sir.<br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />Alright, alright. Not a drop a patience to spare between the two of ya. So, there’s a lot of voices here. 166 different goddamned voices, and each one of them gets to say a little something. They do a good job too, these voices. I’ll give ‘em that much. <br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />What Mr. McDowell is trying to convey is that the diverse and star-studded cast does a very stunning performance of a book filled with emotional range. <br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />Precisely. And should I tell 'em about the tower?<br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />If you’ll, perhaps, permit me?<br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />Yeah, yeah.<br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />Coming into the world the esteemed Mr. Saunders has crafted is indeed disorienting, but can one truly expect anything other than disorientation from the period after death? The flurry of voices, each telling their own tale of misery, woe, regret, desperation, joy, and so forth can make for confusion. But then, as we listened, the voices began to, well<br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />Began to coalesce at the same time as they began to differentiate.<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />And right there in shitting front of me.<br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />Formed in our minds’ eye.<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />Stood a tower of mouths arrayed beside and stacked on top of one another like bricks, each one speaking in turn and<br /><i>matthew </i><br /><br />Having some good goddamned conversation. <br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />It’s surreal structure reflecting the peculiar, surreal imagery of <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i>. A tower that might stand alongside Mr. Vollman’s perpetually engorged appendage and be known to have come from the same place. <br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />These voices forming a cacophony that drew me away from life’s daily trials during my sojourns to and from my work in the hospital. Forming—<br /><i>matthew </i><br /><br />Forming a goddamned great novel.<br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />Forming a tale of such impressive structure, wonder, surprising humour, and depth of emotion that it’s very description defies the abilities of this humble trio.<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />I hope you will permit me the suggestion that it is the finest audiobook that I have ever had the pleasure of hearing. Indeed, I would go so far as to say that it would be a distinct pleasure to be able to listen to the esteemed Mr. Saunders’ writing again. Or, perhaps—<br /><i>matthew </i><br /><br />Or perhaps read the goddamned thing instead of listening to the goddamned thing?<br /><i>mr.mcdowell</i><br /><br />Though I doubt he’d put it in such words, I can’t help but think that is what he means. <br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />Yes, that’s quite right, and your clarification of Mr. McDowell’s most crude nature is appreciated by myself and, no doubt, the audience. <br /><i>matthew </i><br /><br />It is both a pleasure and my duty.<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />Indeed, I could easily see myself reading the novel if only to experience it in another form.<br /><i>matthew </i><br /><br />Alright, enough of all this, it’s time to bring this goddamned experiment to it’s righteous goddamned end.<br /><i>mr.mcdowell</i><br /><br />But Mr. McDowell! We haven’t even begun to discuss the characters, the resonance of the tale with the modern era, the universality of its themes, the diversity of experience represented within—<br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />the civil war as backdrop, the imaginative and playful imagery, the stunning writing, the inclusion of historical documents, the fantastical coexisting with the real and—indeed— how the real and the fantastical serve to elevate one another and<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />and we have not even begun to discuss the story or the inclusion of Willie Lincoln or—<br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />None of that shit matters. What, you want to spoil the whole thing for them? You want their goddamned experience just so yous can talk about it? Ain't it enough just to say that it's good?<br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br />Mr. Quann’s suggestions, though coarse, are perhaps true. <br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />Indeed Matthew, I think it best if we leave it to the listeners and readers to decide what to make of the book. We can invite discussion.<br /><i>mr. quann</i><br /><br />We have certainly enjoyed enough discussion of our own that I would not be surprised to find others enjoying the same. I think Mr. McDowell is correct in that we should leave the discovery of the book to the readers themselves.<br /><i>matthew</i><br /><br />Goddamned right.<br /><i>mr. mcdowell</i><br /><br /><br />**A note to readers who have not read this novel: this review apes the conversational/oral history style of <i>Lincoln in the Bardo</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6629060411944032297" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1936941131" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1936941131" />
    <a title="Phrynne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8655084-phrynne"><img alt="Phrynne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577753809p2/8655084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1936941131?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phrynne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phrynne" href="/user/show/8655084-phrynne">Phrynne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8655084-phrynne?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1936941131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2763863132555313767">This was one of the most unusual books I have ever read! I think it is what you would have to describe as a reading experience since it is told in multiple voices aided by constant footnotes attributing the text to its sources. So clever! And so much research. The author must have become a real expert on Abraham Lincoln by the time he finished writing.<br/>Amazingly the whole fascinating book takes place over one night immediately after Lincoln's young son's funeral. Lincoln makes a last visit to his</span>
  <span id="freeText2763863132555313767" style="display:none">This was one of the most unusual books I have ever read! I think it is what you would have to describe as a reading experience since it is told in multiple voices aided by constant footnotes attributing the text to its sources. So clever! And so much research. The author must have become a real expert on Abraham Lincoln by the time he finished writing.<br />Amazingly the whole fascinating book takes place over one night immediately after Lincoln's young son's funeral. Lincoln makes a last visit to his son and here the book takes a turn into the supernatural and the reader meets a host of people who reside in the cemetary, not believing they are dead, and therefore not passing over as they should.<br />At times the book is weird, at times very sad but always enthralling. I loved it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2763863132555313767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

		    <b>
          <a title="2020 &amp; 2021 Reading Challenge" class="actionLink" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21607588-lincoln-in-the-bardo">2020 &amp; 2021 Readi...:</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zY21UV7ViLb4je+jXFa7EqldtF78G9WhuNWMAr+If9Ikgs5Uj0nGUyAEkw85zkXwKcXYFRn4pL+fHnAGmYZnxg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5nlLgilis87EcBkZ6m9fB3vyOT8Qpm6bbDP6uOyvAAoPdjCH+P79Kxz5ZbWP96Hl+2pVdPVFH4VL+Aa8yqEYHg==')})
  }

When Caesar, a recent arrival from Virginia, tells her about the Underground Railroad, they decide to take a terrifying risk and escape. Matters do not go as planned—Cora kills a young white boy who tries to capture her. Though they manage to find a station and head north, they are being hunted.\n\nIn Whitehead’s ingenious conception, the Underground Railroad is no mere metaphor—engineers and conductors operate a secret network of tracks and tunnels beneath the Southern soil. Cora and Caesar’s first stop is South Carolina, in a city that initially seems like a haven. But the city’s placid surface masks an insidious scheme designed for its black denizens. And even worse: Ridgeway, the relentless slave catcher, is close on their heels. Forced to flee again, Cora embarks on a harrowing flight, state by state, seeking true freedom.\n\nLike the protagonist of Gulliver’s Travels, Cora encounters different worlds at each stage of her journey—hers is an odyssey through time as well as space. As Whitehead brilliantly re-creates the unique terrors for black people in the pre–Civil War era, his narrative seamlessly weaves the saga of America from the brutal importation of Africans to the unfulfilled promises of the present day. The Underground Railroad is at once a kinetic adventure tale of one woman’s ferocious will to escape the horrors of bondage and a shattering, powerful meditation on the history we all share.<\/span>\n It challenges the sacred tenets of the United States Constitution, urban life, the civil rights movement, the father-son relationship, and the holy grail of racial equality―the black Chinese restaurant.\n\nBorn in the &quot;agrarian ghetto&quot; of Dickens―on the southern outskirts of Los Angeles―the narrator of The Sellout resigns himself to the fate of lower-middle-class Californians: &quot;I\'d die in the same bedroom I\'d grown up in, looking up at the cracks in the stucco ceiling that\'ve been there since \'68 quake.&quot; Raised by a single father, a controversial sociologist, he spent his childhood as the subject in racially charged psychological studies. He is led to believe that his father\'s pioneering work will result in a memoir that will solve his family\'s financial woes. But when his father is killed in a police shoot-out, he realizes there never was a memoir. All that\'s left is the bill for a drive-thru funeral.\n\nFueled by this deceit and the general disrepair of his hometown, the narrator sets out to right another wrong: Dickens has literally been removed from the map to save California from further embarrassment. Enlisting the help of the town\'s most famous resident―the last surviving Little Rascal, Hominy Jenkins―he initiates the most outrageous action conceivable: reinstating slavery and segregating the local high school, which lands him in the Supreme Court.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11359743599090963905\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n Drawing on Morrison and Faulkner, The Odyssey and the Old Testament, Ward gives us an epochal story, a journey through Mississippi’s past and present that is both an intimate portrait of a family and an epic tale of hope and struggle. Ward is a major American writer, multiply awarded and universally lauded, and in Sing, Unburied, Sing she is at the height of her powers. Jojo and his toddler sister, Kayla, live with their grandparents, Mam and Pop, and the occasional presence of their drug-addicted mother, Leonie, on a farm on the Gulf Coast of Mississippi. Leonie is simultaneously tormented and comforted by visions of her dead brother, which only come to her when she’s high; Mam is dying of cancer; and quiet, steady Pop tries to run the household and teach Jojo how to be a man. When the white father of Leonie’s children is released from prison, she packs her kids and a friend into her car and sets out across the state for Parchman farm, the Mississippi State Penitentiary, on a journey rife with danger and promise. Sing, Unburied, Sing grapples with the ugly truths at the heart of the American story and the power, and limitations, of the bonds of family. Rich with Ward’s distinctive, musical language, Sing, Unburied, Sing is a majestic new work and an essential contribution to American literature.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6988778559128859244\" Although Bennie and Sasha never discover each other’s pasts, the reader does, in intimate detail, along with the secret lives of a host of other characters whose paths intersect with theirs, over many years, in locales as varied as New York, San Francisco, Naples, and Africa.\n\nWe first meet Sasha in her mid-thirties, on her therapist’s couch in New York City, confronting her long-standing compulsion to steal. Later, we learn the genesis of her turmoil when we see her as the child of a violent marriage, then as a runaway living in Naples, then as a college student trying to avert the suicidal impulses of her best friend. We plunge into the hidden yearnings and disappointments of her uncle, an art historian stuck in a dead marriage, who travels to Naples to extract Sasha from the city’s demimonde and experiences an epiphany of his own while staring at a sculpture of Orpheus and Eurydice in the Museo Nazionale. We meet Bennie Salazar at the melancholy nadir of his adult life—divorced, struggling to connect with his nine-year-old son, listening to a washed-up band in the basement of a suburban house—and then revisit him in 1979, at the height of his youth, shy and tender, reveling in San Francisco’s punk scene as he discovers his ardor for rock and roll and his gift for spotting talent. We learn what became of his high school gang—who thrived and who faltered—and we encounter Lou Kline, Bennie’s catastrophically careless mentor, along with the lovers and children left behind in the wake of Lou’s far-flung sexual conquests and meteoric rise and fall. \n\nA Visit from the Goon Squad is a book about the interplay of time and music, about survival, about the stirrings and transformations set inexorably in motion by even the most passing conjunction of our fates. In a breathtaking array of styles and tones ranging from tragedy to satire to PowerPoint, Egan captures the undertow of self-destruction that we all must either master or succumb to; the basic human hunger for redemption; and the universal tendency to reach for both—and escape the merciless progress of time—in the transporting realms of art and music. Sly, startling, exhilarating work from one of our boldest writers.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16971014273083067807\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8885.George_Saunders">About George Saunders</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8885.George_Saunders"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1588859347p3/8885.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8375341020259735663">George Saunders was born December 2, 1958 and raised on the south side of Chicago. In 1981 he received a B.S. in Geophysical Engineering from Colorado School of Mines in Golden, Colorado. He worked at Radian International, an environmental engineering firm in Rochester, NY as a technical writer and geophysical engineer from 1989 to 1996. He has also worked in Sumatra on an oil exploration geophysi</span>
  <span id="freeText8375341020259735663" style="display:none">George Saunders was born December 2, 1958 and raised on the south side of Chicago. In 1981 he received a B.S. in Geophysical Engineering from Colorado School of Mines in Golden, Colorado. He worked at Radian International, an environmental engineering firm in Rochester, NY as a technical writer and geophysical engineer from 1989 to 1996. He has also worked in Sumatra on an oil exploration geophysics crew, as a doorman in Beverly Hills, a roofer in Chicago, a convenience store clerk, a guitarist in a Texas country-and-western band, and a knuckle-puller in a West Texas slaughterhouse. <br /><br />After reading in <i>People</i> magazine about the Master's program at Syracuse University, he applied. Mr. Saunders received an MA with an emphasis in creative writing in 1988. His thesis advisor was Doug Unger.<br /><br />He has been an Assistant Professor, Syracuse University Creative Writing Program since 1997. He has also been a Visiting Writer at Vermont Studio Center, University of Georgia MayMester Program, University of Denver, University of Texas at Austin, St. Petersburg Literary Seminar (St. Petersburg, Russia, Summer 2000), Brown University, Dickinson College, Hobart &amp; William Smith Colleges. <br /><br />He conducted a Guest Workshop at the Eastman School of Music, Fall 1995, and was an Adjunct Professor at Saint John Fisher College, Rochester, New York, 1990-1995; and Adjunct Professor at Siena College, Loudonville, New York in Fall 1989.<br /><br />He is married and has two children.<br /> <br />His favorite charity is a project to educate Tibetan refugee children in Nepal. Information on this can be found at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.tibetan-buddhist.org/index1.htm">http://www.tibetan-buddhist.org/index...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8375341020259735663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/50281866-lincoln-in-the-bardo">Quotes from Lincoln in the Bardo</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;His mind was freshly inclined toward <i>sorrow</i>; toward the fact that the world was full of sorrow; that everyone labored under some burden of sorrow; that all were suffering; that whatever way one took in this world, one must try to remember that all were suffering (none content; all wronged, neglected, overlooked, misunderstood), and therefore one must do what one could to lighten the load of those with whom one came into contact; that his current state of sorrow was not uniquely his, not at all, but, rather, its like had been felt, would be felt, by scores of others, in all times, in every time, and must not be prolonged or exaggerated, because, in this state, he could be of no help to anyone and, given that his position in the world situated him to be either of great help, or great harm, it would not do to stay low, if he could help it.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/8263735-his-mind-was-freshly-inclined-toward-sorrow-toward-the-fact">112 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Everything was real; inconceivably real, infinitely dear. These and all things started as nothing, latent within a vast energy-broth, but then we named them, and loved them, and, in this way, brought them forth. And now we must lose them.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/8258120-everything-was-real-inconceivably-real-infinitely-dear-these-and-all">90 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/50281866-lincoln-in-the-bardo">More quotes&hellip;</a>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
